<p>The <b>house sparrow</b> (<i>Passer domesticus</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">sparrow</a> family Passeridae, found in most parts of the world. It is a small bird that has a typical length of 16&#160;cm (6.3&#160;in) and a mass of 24–39.5&#160;g (0.85–1.39&#160;oz). Females and young birds are coloured pale brown and grey, and males have brighter black, white, and brown markings. One of about 25&#160;<a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Passer" title="Passer">Passer</a></i>, the house sparrow is native to most of Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>, and a large part of Asia. Its intentional or accidental <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introductions</a> to many regions, including parts of Australasia, Africa, and the Americas, make it the most widely distributed wild bird.
</p><p>The house sparrow is strongly associated with human habitation, and can live in urban or rural settings. Though found in widely varied habitats and climates, it typically avoids extensive <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a> away from human development. It feeds mostly on the seeds of grains and weeds, but it is an opportunistic eater and commonly eats insects and many other foods. Its predators include <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">domestic cats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a>, and many other predatory birds and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>.
</p><p>Because of its numbers, ubiquity, and association with human settlements, the house sparrow is culturally prominent. It is extensively, and usually unsuccessfully, persecuted as an agricultural pest. It has also often been kept as a pet, as well as being a food item and a symbol of lust, sexual potency, commonness, and vulgarity. Though it is widespread and abundant, its numbers have declined in some areas. The animal's <a href="/wiki/Conservation_status" title="Conservation status">conservation status</a> is listed as <a href="/wiki/Least-concern_species" title="Least-concern species">least concern</a> on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Description"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Description</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurements_and_shape">Measurements and shape</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Measurements and shape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow is typically about 16&#160;cm (6.3&#160;in) long, ranging from 14 to 18&#160;cm (5.5 to 7.1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow is a compact bird with a full chest and a large, rounded head. Its bill is stout and conical with a <a href="/wiki/Culmen_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Culmen (bird)">culmen</a> length of 1.1–1.5&#160;cm (0.43–0.59&#160;in), strongly built as an adaptation for eating seeds. Its tail is short, at 5.2–6.5&#160;cm (2.0–2.6&#160;in) long. The <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> is 6.7–8.9&#160;cm (2.6–3.5&#160;in), and the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> is 1.6–2.5&#160;cm (0.63–0.98&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993443_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993443-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Wingspan ranges from 19–25 centimetres (7.5–9.8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In mass, the house sparrow ranges from 24 to 39.5&#160;g (0.85 to 1.39&#160;oz). Females usually are slightly smaller than males. The median mass on the European continent for both sexes is about 30&#160;g (1.1&#160;oz), and in more southerly subspecies is around 26&#160;g (0.92&#160;oz). Younger birds are smaller, males are larger during the winter, and females are larger during the breeding season. Birds at higher latitudes, colder climates, and sometimes higher altitudes are larger (under <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a>), both between and within <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988118–121_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988118–121-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-evo-III_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evo-III-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plumage">Plumage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Plumage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:154px;max-width:154px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:118px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_male_nuptial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Passer_domesticus_male_nuptial.jpg/152px-Passer_domesticus_male_nuptial.jpg" decoding="async" width="152" height="118" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Passer_domesticus_male_nuptial.jpg/228px-Passer_domesticus_male_nuptial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Passer_domesticus_male_nuptial.jpg/304px-Passer_domesticus_male_nuptial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="708" data-file-height="550" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:154px;max-width:154px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:118px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_male_internuptial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Passer_domesticus_male_internuptial.jpg/152px-Passer_domesticus_male_internuptial.jpg" decoding="async" width="152" height="118" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Passer_domesticus_male_internuptial.jpg/228px-Passer_domesticus_male_internuptial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Passer_domesticus_male_internuptial.jpg/304px-Passer_domesticus_male_internuptial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="708" data-file-height="550" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Male house sparrows in breeding (left) and nonbreeding (right) plumage</div></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> of the house sparrow is mostly different shades of grey and brown. The sexes exhibit strong <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">dimorphism</a>: the female is mostly <a href="/wiki/Buff_(colour)" title="Buff (colour)">buffish</a> above and below, while the male has boldly coloured head markings, a reddish back, and grey underparts.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The male has a dark grey <a href="/wiki/Crown_(anatomy)" title="Crown (anatomy)">crown</a> from the top of its bill to its back, and chestnut brown flanking its crown on the sides of its head. It has black around its bill, on its throat, and on the spaces between its bill and eyes (<a href="/wiki/Lore_(anatomy)" title="Lore (anatomy)">lores</a>). It has a small white stripe between the lores and crown and small white spots immediately behind the eyes (postoculars), with black patches below and above them. The underparts are pale grey or white, as are the cheeks, ear <a href="/wiki/Covert_(feather)" class="mw-redirect" title="Covert (feather)">coverts</a>, and stripes at the base of the head. The upper back and mantle are a warm brown, with broad black streaks, while the lower back, rump and upper tail coverts are greyish brown.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg/220px-Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg/330px-Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg/440px-Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="805" data-file-height="544" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plumage_house_sparrow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of the wing of a male</div></div></div>
<p>The male is duller in fresh nonbreeding plumage, with whitish tips on many feathers. Wear and preening expose many of the bright brown and black markings, including most of the black throat and chest patch, called the "bib" or "badge".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006202–203_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006202–203-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The badge is variable in width and general size, and may signal social status or fitness. This hypothesis has led to a "veritable 'cottage industry'" of studies, which have only conclusively shown that patches increase in size with age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006224–225,_244–245_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006224–225,_244–245-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The male's bill is black in the breeding season and dark grey during the rest of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:155px;max-width:155px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:95px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_male_head_and_plumage_(Germany,Eppelheim).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Passer_domesticus_male_head_and_plumage_%28Germany%2CEppelheim%29.jpg/153px-Passer_domesticus_male_head_and_plumage_%28Germany%2CEppelheim%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="153" height="96" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Passer_domesticus_male_head_and_plumage_%28Germany%2CEppelheim%29.jpg/230px-Passer_domesticus_male_head_and_plumage_%28Germany%2CEppelheim%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Passer_domesticus_male_head_and_plumage_%28Germany%2CEppelheim%29.jpg/306px-Passer_domesticus_male_head_and_plumage_%28Germany%2CEppelheim%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1125" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:153px;max-width:153px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:95px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Passer_domesticus_015.jpg/151px-Passer_domesticus_015.jpg" decoding="async" width="151" height="95" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Passer_domesticus_015.jpg/227px-Passer_domesticus_015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Passer_domesticus_015.jpg/302px-Passer_domesticus_015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4194" data-file-height="2642" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Heads of a male (left) and an immature or female (right)</div></div></div></div>
<p>The female has no black markings or grey crown. Its upperparts and head are brown with darker streaks around the mantle and a distinct pale <a href="/wiki/Supercilium" title="Supercilium">supercilium</a>. Its underparts are pale grey-brown. The female's bill is brownish-grey and becomes darker in breeding plumage approaching the black of the male's bill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Juveniles are similar to the adult female, but deeper brown below and paler above, with paler and less defined supercilia. Juveniles have broader buff feather edges, and tend to have looser, scruffier plumage, like moulting adults. Juvenile males tend to have darker throats and white postoculars like adult males, while juvenile females tend to have white throats. However, juveniles cannot be reliably sexed by plumage: some juvenile males lack any markings of the adult male, and some juvenile females have male features. The bills of young birds are light yellow to <a href="/wiki/Straw_(colour)" title="Straw (colour)">straw</a>, paler than the female's bill. Immature males have paler versions of the adult male's markings, which can be very indistinct in fresh plumage. By their first breeding season, young birds generally are indistinguishable from other adults, though they may still be paler during their first year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988116–117-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voice">Voice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Voice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;355040&quot; data-framerate=&quot;25&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Chirping of house sparrow.webm" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Chirping_of_house_sparrow.webm/220px--Chirping_of_house_sparrow.webm.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Chirping_of_house_sparrow.webm" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chirping_of_house_sparrow.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Short chirps of a house sparrow, Hyderabad, India</div></div></div>
<p>Most house sparrow <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">vocalisations</a> are variations on its short and incessant chirping call. Transcribed as <i>chirrup</i>, <i>tschilp</i>, or <i>philip</i>, this note is made as a contact call by flocking or resting birds, or by males to proclaim nest ownership and invite pairing. In the breeding season, the male gives this call repetitively, with emphasis and speed, but not much rhythm, forming what is described either as a song or an "ecstatic call" similar to a song.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196326–30_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196326–30-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampPerrins1994291_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampPerrins1994291-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Young birds also give a true song, especially in captivity, a warbling similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/European_greenfinch" title="European greenfinch">European greenfinch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963101_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963101-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aggressive males give a trilled version of their call, transcribed as "chur-<i>chur-r-r-it-it-it-it</i>". This call is also used by females in the breeding season, to establish dominance over males while displacing them to feed young or incubate eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196330–31_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196330–31-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> House sparrows give a nasal alarm call, the basic sound of which is transcribed as <i>quer</i>, and a shrill <i>chree</i> call in great distress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196331–32_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196331–32-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Another vocalisation is the "appeasement call", a soft <i>quee</i> given to inhibit aggression, usually given between birds of a mated pair.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196330–31_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196330–31-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> These vocalisations are not unique to the house sparrow, but are shared, with small variations, by all sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988254_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988254-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variation">Variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_(Passer_domesticus)-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg/220px-House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg/330px-House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg/440px-House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="458" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_(Passer_domesticus)-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A male of the Indian subspecies (<i>P. d. indicus</i>) in Kolkata, India</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Indian_sparrow_(Passer_domesticus_indicus)_immature_Udaipur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Indian_sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus_indicus%29_immature_Udaipur.jpg/170px-Indian_sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus_indicus%29_immature_Udaipur.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Indian_sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus_indicus%29_immature_Udaipur.jpg/255px-Indian_sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus_indicus%29_immature_Udaipur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Indian_sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus_indicus%29_immature_Udaipur.jpg/340px-Indian_sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus_indicus%29_immature_Udaipur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2872" data-file-height="2872" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Indian_sparrow_(Passer_domesticus_indicus)_immature_Udaipur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An immature of the Indian subspecies (<i>P. d. indicus</i>) in Rajasthan, India</div></div></div>
<p>Some variation is seen in the 12 subspecies of house sparrows, which are divided into two groups, the <a href="/wiki/Indomalayan_realm" title="Indomalayan realm">Oriental</a> <i>P. d. indicus</i> group, and the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic_realm" title="Palearctic realm">Palaearctic</a> <i>P. d. domesticus</i> group. Birds of the <i>P. d. domesticus</i> group have grey cheeks, while <i>P. d. indicus</i> group birds have white cheeks, as well as bright colouration on the crown, a smaller bill, and a longer black bib.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The subspecies <i>P. d. tingitanus</i> differs little from the nominate subspecies, except in the worn breeding plumage of the male, in which the head is speckled with black and underparts are paler.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988117_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988117-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <i>P. d. balearoibericus</i> is slightly paler than the nominate, but darker than <i>P. d. bibilicus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnowPerrins19981061–1064_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnowPerrins19981061–1064-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <i>P. d. bibilicus</i> is paler than most subspecies, but has the grey cheeks of <i>P. d. domesticus</i> group birds. The similar <i>P. d. persicus</i> is paler and smaller, and <i>P. d. niloticus</i> is nearly identical but smaller. Of the less widespread <i>P. d. indicus</i> group subspecies, <i>P. d. hyrcanus</i> is larger than <i>P. d. indicus</i>, <i>P. d. hufufae</i> is paler, <i>P. d. bactrianus</i> is larger and paler, and <i>P. d. parkini</i> is larger and darker with more black on the breast than any other subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988117_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988117-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993445_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993445-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts1992472–477_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts1992472–477-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identification">Identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow can be confused with a number of other seed-eating birds, especially its relatives in the genus <i>Passer</i>. Many of these relatives are smaller, with an appearance that is neater or "cuter", as with the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_sparrow" title="Dead Sea sparrow">Dead Sea sparrow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMullarneySvenssonZetterstromGrant1999342–343_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMullarneySvenssonZetterstromGrant1999342–343-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The dull-coloured female can often not be distinguished from other females, and is nearly identical to those of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_sparrow" title="Spanish sparrow">Spanish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_sparrow" title="Italian sparrow">Italian sparrows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a> is smaller and slenderer with a chestnut crown and a black patch on each cheek.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993463–465_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993463–465-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The male Spanish sparrow and Italian sparrow are distinguished by their chestnut crowns. The <a href="/wiki/Sind_sparrow" title="Sind sparrow">Sind sparrow</a> is very similar but smaller, with less black on the male's throat and a distinct pale supercilium on the female.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClementHarrisDavis1993444-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_systematics">Taxonomy and systematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and systematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow was among the first animals to be given a <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">scientific name</a> in the modern system of <a href="/wiki/Biological_classification" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological classification">biological classification</a>, since it was described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a>, in the 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>. It was described from a <a href="/wiki/Type_specimen" class="mw-redirect" title="Type specimen">type specimen</a> collected in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, with the name <i>Fringilla domestica</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988121–122_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988121–122-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELinnaeus1758183_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinnaeus1758183-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Later, the genus name <i><a href="/wiki/Fringilla" title="Fringilla">Fringilla</a></i> came to be used only for the <a href="/wiki/Common_chaffinch" title="Common chaffinch">common chaffinch</a> and its relatives, and the house sparrow has usually been placed in the genus <i>Passer</i> created by French zoologist <a href="/wiki/Mathurin_Jacques_Brisson" title="Mathurin Jacques Brisson">Mathurin Jacques Brisson</a> in 1760.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988114–115_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988114–115-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrisson176036_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrisson176036-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The bird's scientific name and its usual English name have the same meaning. The <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>passer</i>, like the English word "sparrow", is a term for small active birds, coming from a root word referring to speed.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith198813_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith198813-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The Latin word <i>domesticus</i> means "belonging to the house", like the common name a reference to its association with humans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJobling2009138_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJobling2009138-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow is also called by a number of alternative English names, including <i>English sparrow</i>, chiefly in North America;<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurcotteWatts1999429_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurcotteWatts1999429-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Indian sparrow</i> or <i>Indian house sparrow</i>, for the birds of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESibleyMonroe1990669–670_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESibleyMonroe1990669–670-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Dialectal names include <i>sparr</i>, <i>sparrer</i>, <i>spadger</i>, <i>spadgick</i>, and <i>philip</i>, mainly in southern England; <i>spug</i> and <i>spuggy</i>, mainly in northern England; <i>spur</i> and <i>sprig</i>, mainly in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood1984114–146_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood1984114–146-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwainson188560–62_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwainson188560–62-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and <i>spatzie</i> or <i>spotsie</i>, from the German <i>Spatz</i>, in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarver1987162,_199_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarver1987162,_199-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_italiae_pair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Passer_italiae_pair.jpg/220px-Passer_italiae_pair.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Passer_italiae_pair.jpg/330px-Passer_italiae_pair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Passer_italiae_pair.jpg/440px-Passer_italiae_pair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1834" data-file-height="1202" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_italiae_pair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of <a href="/wiki/Italian_sparrow" title="Italian sparrow">Italian sparrows</a>, in Rome</div></div></div>
<p>The genus <i>Passer</i> contains about 25 species, depending on the authority, 26 according to the <i><a href="/wiki/Handbook_of_the_Birds_of_the_World" title="Handbook of the Birds of the World">Handbook of the Birds of the World</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Most <i>Passer</i> species are dull-coloured birds with short, square tails and stubby, conical beaks, between 11 and 18&#160;cm (4.3 and 7.1&#160;in) long.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988253–254_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988253–254-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">Mitochondrial DNA</a> studies suggest that <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a> in the genus occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> and earlier, while other evidence suggests speciation occurred 25,000 to 15,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Within <i>Passer</i>, the house sparrow is part of the "<a href="/wiki/Palaearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palaearctic">Palaearctic</a> black-bibbed sparrows" group and a close relative of the Mediterranean "willow sparrows".<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The taxonomy of the house sparrow and its Mediterranean relatives is complicated. The common type of "willow sparrow" is the Spanish sparrow, which resembles the house sparrow in many respects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988164_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988164-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> It frequently prefers wetter habitats than the house sparrow, and it is often colonial and nomadic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988172_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988172-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In most of the Mediterranean, one or both species occur, with some degree of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson200616_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson200616-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In North Africa, the two species hybridise extensively, forming highly variable mixed populations with a full range of characters from pure house sparrows to pure Spanish sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–127_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–127-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Töpfer_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Töpfer-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In most of Italy, the breeding species is the <a href="/wiki/Italian_sparrow" title="Italian sparrow">Italian sparrow</a>, which has an appearance intermediate between those of the house and Spanish sparrows. Its specific status and origin are the subject of much debate, but it may be a case of long-ago <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_speciation" title="Hybrid speciation">hybrid speciation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Töpfer_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Töpfer-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson200613–18,_25–26_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson200613–18,_25–26-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, the Italian sparrow <a href="/wiki/Intergrade" class="mw-redirect" title="Intergrade">intergrades</a> over a narrow roughly 20&#160;km (12&#160;mi) strip with the house sparrow, and some house sparrows migrate into the Italian sparrow's range in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988121–126_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988121–126-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> On the Mediterranean islands of <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gozo" title="Gozo">Gozo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karpathos" title="Karpathos">Karpathos</a>, other apparently intermediate birds are of unknown status.<sup id="cite_ref-Töpfer_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Töpfer-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988169–170_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988169–170-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith199222,_27_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith199222,_27-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A male of subspecies <i>P. d. balearoibericus</i> in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg/220px-Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg/330px-Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg/440px-Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3140" data-file-height="2355" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_montanus_Baikonur_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A male of the migratory subspecies <i>P. d. bactrianus</i> (with a <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a> and young house or Spanish sparrows) in <a href="/wiki/Baikonur" title="Baikonur">Baikonur</a>, Kazakhstan</div></div></div>
<p>A large number of subspecies have been named, of which 12 were recognised in the <i>Handbook of the Birds of the World</i>. These subspecies are divided into two groups, the Palaearctic <i>P. d. domesticus</i> group, and the Oriental <i>P. d. indicus</i> group.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Several Middle Eastern subspecies, including <i>P. d. biblicus</i>, are sometimes considered a third, intermediate group. The subspecies <i>P. d. indicus</i> was described as a species, and was considered to be distinct by many ornithologists during the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Migratory birds of the subspecies <i>P. d. bactrianus</i> in the <i>P. d. indicus</i> group were recorded overlapping with <i>P. d. domesticus</i> birds without hybridising in the 1970s, so the Soviet scientists Edward I. Gavrilov and M. N. Korelov proposed the separation of the <i>P. d. indicus</i> group as a separate species.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988114–115_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988114–115-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, <i>P. d. indicus</i> group and <i>P. d. domesticus</i> group birds intergrade in a large part of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, so this split is rarely recognised.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In North America, house sparrow populations are more differentiated than those in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-evo-III_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evo-III-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This variation follows predictable patterns, with birds at higher latitudes being larger and darker and those in arid areas being smaller and paler.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOberholser19741009_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOberholser19741009-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> However,  how much this is caused by evolution or by environment is not clear.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Similar observations have been made in New Zealand<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>and in South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988133–135_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988133–135-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The introduced house sparrow populations may be distinct enough to merit subspecies status, especially in North America and southern Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and American ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Harry_Church_Oberholser" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Church Oberholser">Harry Church Oberholser</a> even gave the subspecies name <i>P. d. plecticus</i> to the paler birds of western North America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOberholser19741009_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOberholser19741009-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt><i>P. d. domesticus</i> group</dt></dl>
<ul><li>The <b>European house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. domesticus</i>) <small>Linnaeus, 1758</small>, the <a href="/wiki/Nominate_subspecies" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominate subspecies">nominate subspecies</a>, is found in most of Europe, across northern Asia to <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a>. It is the most widely introduced subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988121–122_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988121–122-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Western Mediterranean house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. balearoibericus</i>) <small>von Jordans, 1923</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Majorca" class="mw-redirect" title="Majorca">Majorca</a>, is found in the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>, southern France, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Northwest African house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. tingitanus</i>) <small>(<a href="/wiki/Victor_Loche" title="Victor Loche">Loche</a>, 1867)</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, is found in the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ajdabiya" title="Ajdabiya">Ajdabiya</a> in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> to <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9ni_Abb%C3%A8s" title="Béni Abbès">Béni Abbès</a> in Algeria, and to <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>'s Atlantic coast. It hybridises extensively with the Spanish sparrow, especially in the eastern part of its range.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Egyptian house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. niloticus</i>) <small>Nicoll and Bonhote, 1909</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Faiyum" title="Faiyum">Faiyum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, is found along the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> north of <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Halfa" title="Wadi Halfa">Wadi Halfa</a>, Sudan. It intergrades with <i>bibilicus</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai</a>, and with <i>rufidorsalis</i> in a narrow zone around <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Halfa" title="Wadi Halfa">Wadi Halfa</a>. It has been recorded in <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackworth-PraedGrant1955870–871_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackworth-PraedGrant1955870–871-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Persian house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. persicus</i>) <small><a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Zarudny" title="Nikolai Zarudny">Zarudny</a> and Kudashev, 1916</small>, described from the <a href="/wiki/Karun_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Karun River">Karun River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Khuzestan" class="mw-redirect" title="Khuzestan">Khuzestan</a>, Iran, is found in the western and central Iran south of the <a href="/wiki/Alborz" title="Alborz">Alborz</a> mountains, intergrading with <i>indicus</i> in eastern Iran, and Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampPerrins1994289_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampPerrins1994289-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Eastern Mediterranean house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. biblicus</i>) <small><a href="/wiki/Ernst_Hartert" title="Ernst Hartert">Hartert</a>, 1910</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, is found in the Middle East from <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and southeastern Turkey to the Sinai in the west and from <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt><i>P. d. indicus</i> group</dt></dl>
<ul><li><i>P. d. hyrcanus</i> <small>Zarudny and Kudashev, 1916</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Gorgan" title="Gorgan">Gorgan</a>, Iran, is found along the southern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> from Gorgan to southeastern Azerbaijan. It intergrades with <i>P. d. persicus</i> in the Alborz Mountains, and with <i>P. d. bibilicus</i> to the west. It is the subspecies with the smallest range.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Turkestan house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. bactrianus</i>) <small>Zarudny and Kudashev, 1916</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a>, is found in southern Kazakhstan to the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> and northern Iran and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. It intergrades with <i>persicus</i> in <a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a> and with <i>indicus</i> across central Afghanistan. Unlike most other house sparrow subspecies, it is almost entirely migratory, wintering in the plains of the northern Indian subcontinent. It is found in open country rather than in settlements, which are occupied by the Eurasian tree sparrow in its range.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> There is an exceptional record from Sudan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackworth-PraedGrant1955870–871_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackworth-PraedGrant1955870–871-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Kashmir house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. parkini</i>) <small><a href="/wiki/Hugh_Whistler" title="Hugh Whistler">Whistler</a>, 1920</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Srinagar" title="Srinagar">Srinagar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, is found in the western <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a> to southeastern <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>. It is migratory, like <i>P. d. bactrianus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Indian house sparrow</b> (<i>P. d. indicus</i>) <small><a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Jardine,_7th_Baronet" title="Sir William Jardine, 7th Baronet">Jardine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prideaux_John_Selby" title="Prideaux John Selby">Selby</a>, 1831</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a>, is found in the Indian subcontinent south of the Himalayas, in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, eastern Iran, southwestern Arabia and southern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. hufufae</i> <small><a href="/wiki/Claud_Buchanan_Ticehurst" class="mw-redirect" title="Claud Buchanan Ticehurst">Ticehurst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Frederic_Cheeseman" title="Thomas Frederic Cheeseman">Cheeseman</a>, 1924</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Hofuf" title="Hofuf">Hofuf</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, is found in northeastern Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. rufidorsalis</i> <small><a href="/wiki/C._L._Brehm" class="mw-redirect" title="C. L. Brehm">C. L. Brehm</a>, 1855</small>, described from <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, is found in the Nile Valley from Wadi Halfa south to <a href="/wiki/Renk,_South_Sudan" title="Renk, South Sudan">Renk</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128_63-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988126–128-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackworth-PraedGrant1955870–871_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackworth-PraedGrant1955870–871-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and in eastern Sudan and northern Ethiopia to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> It has also been introduced to <a href="/wiki/Moh%C3%A9li" title="Mohéli">Mohéli</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988134_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988134-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG/170px-House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG/255px-House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG/340px-House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1294" data-file-height="1727" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>By a nest in a <a href="/wiki/Saguaro" title="Saguaro">saguaro</a> cactus in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook,_New_Zealand.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook,_New_Zealand.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>House sparrows perching on a roof, during winter in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Alps_(New_Zealand)" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Alps (New Zealand)">Southern Alps</a> of New Zealand</div></div></div>
<p>The house sparrow originated in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and spread, along with agriculture, to most of Eurasia and parts of North Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson20065,_9–12_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson20065,_9–12-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Since the mid-19th century, it has reached most of the world, chiefly due to deliberate introductions, but also through natural and shipborne dispersal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988129–137,_280–283_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988129–137,_280–283-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Its introduced range encompasses most of North America, <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, southern South America, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>, part of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and islands throughout the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson20065_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson20065-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It has greatly extended its range in northern Eurasia since the 1850s,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963171–173_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963171–173-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and continues to do so, as was shown by its colonisation around 1990 of <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rishiri_Island" title="Rishiri Island">Rishiri Island</a>, Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson200622_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson200622-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The extent of its range makes it the most widely distributed wild bird on the planet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson20065_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson20065-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introductions">Introductions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Introductions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow has become highly successful in most parts of the world where it has been introduced. This is mostly due to its early adaptation to living with humans, and its adaptability to a wide range of conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988293–296_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988293–296-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Other factors may include its robust immune response, compared to the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Where introduced, it can extend its range quickly, sometimes at a rate over 230&#160;km (140&#160;mi) per year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakersDaviesReilly1984586_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakersDaviesReilly1984586-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In many parts of the world, it has been characterised as a pest, and poses a threat to native birds.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> A few introductions have died out or been of limited success, such as those to <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first of many successful introductions to North America occurred when birds from England were released in New York City, in 1852,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrows188917_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrows188917-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Healy_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Healy-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> intended to control the ravages of the <a href="/wiki/Ellida_caniplaga" title="Ellida caniplaga">linden moth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In North America, the house sparrow now occurs from the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a> of Canada to southern <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and it is one of the most abundant birds of the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow was first introduced to Australia in 1863 at <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> and is common throughout the eastern part of the continent as far north as <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_Peninsula" title="Cape York Peninsula">Cape York</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> but has been prevented from establishing itself in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> where every house sparrow found in the state is killed.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> House sparrows were introduced in New Zealand in 1859, and from there reached many of the Pacific islands, including <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson200625_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson200625-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In southern Africa, birds of both the European subspecies (<i>P. d. domesticus</i>) and the Indian subspecies (<i>P. d. indicus</i>) were introduced around 1900. Birds of <i>P. d. domesticus</i> ancestry are confined to a few towns, while <i>P. d. indicus</i> birds have spread rapidly, reaching <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> in the 1980s. Despite this rapid spread, native relatives such as the <a href="/wiki/Cape_sparrow" title="Cape sparrow">Cape sparrow</a> also occur and thrive in urban habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In South America, it was first introduced near <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> around 1870, and quickly became common in most of the southern part of the continent. It now occurs almost continuously from <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> to the fringes of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a>, with isolated populations as far north as coastal Venezuela.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELever2005210–212_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELever2005210–212-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERestallRodnerLentino2007777_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERestallRodnerLentino2007777-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow is closely associated with human habitation and cultivation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> It is not an obligate <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensal</a> of humans as some have suggested: birds of the migratory Central Asian subspecies usually breed away from humans in open country,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006424–425_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006424–425-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> and birds elsewhere are occasionally found away from humans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The only terrestrial habitats that the house sparrow does not inhabit are dense forest and <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>. Well adapted to living around humans, it frequently lives and even breeds indoors, especially in factories, warehouses, and zoos.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> It has been recorded breeding in an English <a href="/wiki/Coal_mine" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal mine">coal mine</a> 640&#160;m (2,100&#160;ft) below ground,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1992128–132_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1992128–132-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and feeding on the <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a>'s observation deck at night.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> It reaches its greatest densities in urban centres, but its reproductive success is greater in suburbs, where insects are more abundant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Poel_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poel-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> On a larger scale, it is most abundant in wheat-growing areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988129_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988129-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It tolerates a variety of climates, but prefers drier conditions, especially in moist tropical climates.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_79-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138_89-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> It has several adaptations to dry areas, including a high salt tolerance<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and an ability to survive without water by ingesting <a href="/wiki/Berry" title="Berry">berries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walsberg_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walsberg-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In most of eastern Asia, the house sparrow is entirely absent, replaced by the Eurasian tree sparrow.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Where these two species overlap, the house sparrow is usually more common than the Eurasian tree sparrow, but one species may replace the other in a manner that ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Maud_Doria_Haviland" title="Maud Doria Haviland">Maud Doria Haviland</a> described as "random, or even capricious".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988228_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988228-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In most of its range, the house sparrow is extremely common, despite some declines,<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> but in marginal habitats such as rainforest or mountain ranges, its distribution can be spotty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138_89-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–138-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;488784&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/94/House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv/House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv.360p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;WebM (360P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 360P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;360p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;480&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;496664&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:House Sparrow dust bath.ogv" style="width:160px;height:120px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv/160px--House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The house sparrow often bathes in water (at left) or in dust (at right)</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_behaviour">Social behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Social behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow is a very social bird. It is gregarious during all seasons when feeding, often forming flocks with other species of birds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006247_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006247-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> It <a href="/wiki/Roosting" class="mw-redirect" title="Roosting">roosts</a> communally and while breeding nests are usually grouped together in clumps. House sparrows also engage in social activities such as <a href="/wiki/Dust_bath" class="mw-redirect" title="Dust bath">dust</a> or water bathing and "social singing", in which birds call together in bushes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow feeds mostly on the ground, but it flocks in trees and bushes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> At feeding stations and nests, female house sparrows are dominant despite their smaller size, and they can fight over males in the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep_and_roosting">Sleep and roosting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sleep and roosting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>House sparrows sleep with the bill tucked underneath the scapular feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Outside of the reproductive season, they often roost communally in trees or shrubs. Much communal chirping occurs before and after the birds settle in the roost in the evening, as well as before the birds leave the roost in the morning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Some congregating sites separate from the roost may be visited by the birds prior to settling in for the night.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Body_maintenance">Body maintenance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Body maintenance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dust or water bathing is common and often occurs in groups. <a href="/wiki/Anting_(bird_activity)" title="Anting (bird activity)">Anting</a> is rare.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Head scratching is done with the leg over the drooped wing.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg/220px-House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg/330px-House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg/440px-House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3582" data-file-height="2160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_feeding_grains.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A female house sparrow feeding on rice grains</div></div></div>
