<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg/220px-House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg/330px-House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg/440px-House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrows_feeding_on_scraps.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two females feeding on leftover food at a cafe in New Zealand.</div></div></div>
<p>As an adult, the house sparrow mostly feeds on the seeds of <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weeds</a>, but it is opportunistic and adaptable, and eats whatever foods are available.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006273–275_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006273–275-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In towns and cities, it often scavenges for food in garbage containers and congregates in the outdoors of restaurants and other eating establishments to feed on leftover food and crumbs. It can perform complex tasks to obtain food, such as opening automatic doors to enter supermarkets,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006246_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006246-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> clinging to hotel walls to watch vacationers on their balconies,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nectar_robbing" title="Nectar robbing">nectar robbing</a> <a href="/wiki/Kowhai" class="mw-redirect" title="Kowhai">kowhai</a> flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In common with many other birds, the house sparrow requires <a href="/wiki/Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith">grit</a> to digest the harder items in its diet. Grit can be either stone, often grains of masonry, or the shells of eggs or snails; oblong and rough grains are preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006279–281_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006279–281-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several studies of the house sparrow in temperate agricultural areas have found the proportion of seeds in its diet to be about 90%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006273–275_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006273–275-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196334–35_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196334–35-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988159–161_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988159–161-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> It will eat almost any seeds, but where it has a choice, it prefers <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">oats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196333_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196333-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In urban areas, the house sparrow feeds largely on food provided directly or indirectly by humans, such as bread, though it prefers raw seeds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988159–161_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988159–161-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow also eats some plant matter besides seeds, including <a href="/wiki/Bud" title="Bud">buds</a>, berries, and fruits such as grapes and cherries.<sup id="cite_ref-Walsberg_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walsberg-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988159–161_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988159–161-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In temperate areas, the house sparrow has an unusual habit of tearing flowers, especially yellow ones, in the spring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196335,_38–39_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196335,_38–39-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Animals form another important part of the house sparrow's diet, chiefly <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, of which <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diptera" class="mw-redirect" title="Diptera">dipteran</a> flies, and <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> are especially important. Various noninsect <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> are eaten, as are <a href="/wiki/Mollusc" class="mw-redirect" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> where available, <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006273–275_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006273–275-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Young house sparrows are fed mostly on insects until about 15 days after hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVincent20052–3_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVincent20052–3-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> They are also given small quantities of seeds, spiders, and grit. In most places, <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cricket_(insect)" title="Cricket (insect)">crickets</a> are the most abundant foods of nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006276–279_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006276–279-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hemiptera" title="Hemiptera">True bugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sawfly" title="Sawfly">sawflies</a>, and beetles are also important, but house sparrows take advantage of whatever foods are abundant to feed their young.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006276–279_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006276–279-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanov_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanov-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> House sparrows have been observed <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">stealing</a> prey from other birds, including <a href="/wiki/American_robin" title="American robin">American robins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The gut microbiota of house sparrows differs between chicks and adults, with <a href="/wiki/Proteobacteria" title="Proteobacteria">Proteobacteria</a> decreasing in chicks when they get to around 9 days old, whilst the relative abundance of <a href="/wiki/Firmicutes" title="Firmicutes">Firmicutes</a> increase.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">Locomotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Locomotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_hopping.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/House_sparrow_hopping.jpg/220px-House_sparrow_hopping.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/House_sparrow_hopping.jpg/330px-House_sparrow_hopping.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/House_sparrow_hopping.jpg/440px-House_sparrow_hopping.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1872" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_hopping.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hopping movements of a house sparrow</div></div></div>
<p>The house sparrow's flight is direct (not undulating) and flapping, averaging 45.5&#160;km/h (28.3&#160;mph) and about 15&#160;wingbeats per second.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> On the ground, the house sparrow typically hops rather than walks. It can swim when pressed to do so by pursuit from predators. Captive birds have been recorded diving and swimming short distances under water.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_107-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersal_and_migration">Dispersal and migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Dispersal and migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most house sparrows do not move more than a few kilometres during their lifetimes. However, limited migration occurs in all regions. Some young birds disperse long distances, especially on coasts, and mountain birds move to lower elevations in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Two subspecies, <i>P. d. bactrianus</i> and <i>P. d. parkini</i>, are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a>. Unlike the birds in sedentary populations that migrate, birds of migratory subspecies prepare for migration by putting on weight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142_102-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988139–142-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg/220px-House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg/330px-House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg/440px-House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of the Indian subspecies (<i>P. d. indicus</i>) mating in <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a></div></div></div>
<p>House sparrows can breed in the breeding season immediately following their hatching, and sometimes attempt to do so. Some birds breeding for the first time in tropical areas are only a few months old and still have juvenile plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006135–136_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006135–136-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Birds breeding for the first time are rarely successful in raising young, and reproductive success increases with age, as older birds breed earlier in the breeding season, and fledge more young.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> As the breeding season approaches, hormone releases trigger enormous increases in the size of the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_organs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual organs">sexual organs</a> and changes in day length lead males to start calling by nesting sites.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirkhead201247–48_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhead201247–48-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The timing of mating and egg-laying varies geographically, and between specific locations and years because a sufficient supply of insects is needed for egg formation and feeding nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Males take up nesting sites before the breeding season, by frequently calling beside them. Unmated males start nest construction and call particularly frequently to attract females. When a female approaches a male during this period, the male <a href="/wiki/Courtship_display" title="Courtship display">displays</a> by moving up and down while drooping and shivering his wings, pushing up his head, raising and spreading his tail, and showing his bib.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Males may try to mate with females while calling or displaying. In response, a female will adopt a threatening posture and attack a male before flying away, pursued by the male. The male displays in front of her, attracting other males, which also pursue and display to the female. This group display usually does not immediately result in copulations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Other males usually do not copulate with the female.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988142–143_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988142–143-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Copulation is typically initiated by the female giving a soft <i>dee-dee-dee</i> call to the male. Birds of a pair <a href="/wiki/Copulation_(zoology)" title="Copulation (zoology)">copulate</a> frequently until the female is laying eggs, and the male mounts the female repeatedly each time a pair mates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The house sparrow is <a href="/wiki/Monogamous" class="mw-redirect" title="Monogamous">monogamous</a>, and typically mates for life, but birds from pairs often engage in <a href="/wiki/Extra-pair_copulation" title="Extra-pair copulation">extra-pair copulations</a>, so about 15% of house sparrow fledglings are unrelated to their mother's mate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006141–142_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006141–142-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Male house sparrows guard their mates carefully to avoid being cuckolded, and most extra-pair copulation occurs away from nest sites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988142–143_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988142–143-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006145_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006145-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Males may sometimes have multiple mates, and bigamy is mostly limited by aggression between females.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006143–144_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006143–144-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Many birds do not find a nest and a mate, and instead may serve as <a href="/wiki/Helpers_at_the_nest" title="Helpers at the nest">helpers</a> around the nest for mated pairs, a role which increases the chances of being chosen to replace a lost mate. Lost mates of both sexes can be replaced quickly during the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988142–143_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988142–143-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The formation of a pair and the bond between the two birds is tied to the holding of a nest site, though paired house sparrows can recognise each other away from the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting">Nesting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nesting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Female bringing food for young in a nest made in a tree hole in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Sparrow in a ventilator" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg/220px-Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg/330px-Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg/440px-Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3347" data-file-height="2619" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sparrow_in_a_ventilator.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sparrow in a ventilator</div></div></div>
<p>Nest sites are varied, though cavities are preferred. Nests are most frequently built in the <a href="/wiki/Eaves" title="Eaves">eaves</a> and other crevices of houses. Holes in cliffs and banks, or <a href="/wiki/Tree_hollow" title="Tree hollow">tree hollows</a>, are also used.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> A sparrow sometimes excavates its own nests in sandy banks or rotten branches, but more frequently uses the nests of other birds such as those of <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallows</a> in banks and cliffs, and old tree cavity nests. It usually uses deserted nests, though sometimes it usurps active ones by driving away or killing the occupants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gowaty_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gowaty-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Tree hollows are more commonly used in North America than in Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> putting the sparrows in competition with <a href="/wiki/Bluebird" title="Bluebird">bluebirds</a> and other North American cavity nesters, and thereby contributing to their population declines.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Especially in warmer areas, the house sparrow may build its nests in the open, on the branches of trees, especially evergreens and hawthorns, or in the nests of large birds such as <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">storks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magpie" title="Magpie">magpies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaverschmidt194933–34_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaverschmidt194933–34-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In open nesting sites, breeding success tends to be lower, since breeding begins late and the nest can easily be destroyed or damaged by storms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorrisTegetmeier18968–9_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrisTegetmeier18968–9-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Less common nesting sites include <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">street lights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neon_sign" title="Neon sign">neon signs</a>, favoured for their warmth; and the old open-topped nests of other songbirds, which are then domed over.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
Usually the couples rpeat copulation many a times and every copulation is followed by some break of 3-4secs and in that time both pair change their position by some distance.
The nest is usually domed, though it may lack a roof in enclosed sites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> It has an outer layer of stems and roots, a middle layer of dead grass and leaves, and a lining of feathers, as well as of paper and other soft materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Nests typically have external dimensions of 20 × 30&#160;cm (8 × 12&#160;in),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> but their size varies greatly.<sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_140-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The building of the nest is initiated by the unmated male while displaying to females. The female assists in building, but is less active than the male.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Some nest building occurs throughout the year, especially after moult in autumn. In colder areas house sparrows build specially created roost nests, or roost in street lights, to avoid losing heat during the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> House sparrows do not hold territories, but they defend their nests aggressively against intruders of the same sex.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57_139-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196352–57-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>House sparrows' nests support a wide range of scavenging insects, including nest flies such as <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neottiophilum_praestum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Neottiophilum praestum (page does not exist)">Neottiophilum praestum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Protocalliphora" title="Protocalliphora">Protocalliphora</a></i> blowflies,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963131–132_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963131–132-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and over 1,400 species of beetle.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs_and_young">Eggs and young</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Eggs and young">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG/220px-Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG/330px-Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG/440px-Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eggs in a nest</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">Clutches</a> usually comprise four or five <a href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a>, though numbers from one to 10  have been recorded. At least two clutches are usually laid, and up to seven a year may be laid in the tropics or four a year in temperate latitudes. When fewer clutches are laid in a year, especially at higher latitudes, the number of eggs per clutch is greater. Central Asian house sparrows, which migrate and have only one clutch a year, average 6.5&#160;eggs in a clutch. Clutch size is also affected by environmental and seasonal conditions, female age, and breeding density.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988148–149_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988148–149-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006157–172_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006157–172-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_chick.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Naked and blind chick" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/House_sparrow_chick.jpg/220px-House_sparrow_chick.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/House_sparrow_chick.jpg/330px-House_sparrow_chick.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/House_sparrow_chick.jpg/440px-House_sparrow_chick.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_chick.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A hatchling</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg/220px-F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg/330px-F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg/440px-F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="801" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A5gel---2020.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dead, about one week old.</div></div></div>
<p>Some intraspecific <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasitism</a> occurs, and instances of unusually large numbers of eggs in a nest may be the result of females laying eggs in the nests of their neighbours. Such foreign eggs are sometimes recognised and ejected by females.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988148–149_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988148–149-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006145–146_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006145–146-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow is a victim of interspecific brood parasites, but only rarely, since it usually uses nests in holes too small for parasites to enter, and it feeds its young foods unsuitable for young parasites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006319_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006319-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies200055_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies200055-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In turn, the house sparrow has once been recorded as a brood parasite of the <a href="/wiki/American_cliff_swallow" class="mw-redirect" title="American cliff swallow">American cliff swallow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006145–146_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006145–146-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House_sparrow_portrait.jpg/220px-House_sparrow_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House_sparrow_portrait.jpg/330px-House_sparrow_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House_sparrow_portrait.jpg/440px-House_sparrow_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile, showing its pink bill and obvious nestling gape—the soft, swollen base, which becomes harder and less swollen as the bird matures</div></div></div>
<p>The eggs are white, bluish white, or greenish white, spotted with brown or grey.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_107-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Subelliptical in shape,<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> they range from 20 to 22&#160;mm (0.79 to 0.87&#160;in) in length and 14 to 16&#160;mm (0.55 to 0.63&#160;in) in width,<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> have an average mass of 2.9&#160;g (0.10&#160;oz),<sup id="cite_ref-BTO_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTO-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> and an average surface area of 9.18&#160;cm<sup>2</sup> (1.423&#160;in<sup>2</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-Paganelli_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paganelli-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Eggs from the tropical subspecies are distinctly smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgilvie-Grant1912201–204_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgilvie-Grant1912201–204-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHumeOates1890169–151_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumeOates1890169–151-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Eggs begin to develop with the deposition of <a href="/wiki/Egg_yolk" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg yolk">yolk</a> in the ovary a few days before ovulation. In the day between ovulation and laying, <a href="/wiki/Egg_white" title="Egg white">egg white</a> forms, followed by <a href="/wiki/Eggshell" title="Eggshell">eggshell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006175–176_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006175–176-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Eggs laid later in a clutch are larger, as are those laid by larger females, and egg size is hereditary. Eggs decrease slightly in size from laying to hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006173–175_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006173–175-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The yolk comprises 25% of the egg, the egg white 68%, and the shell 7%. Eggs are watery, being 79% liquid, and otherwise mostly protein.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006176–177_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006176–177-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The female develops a <a href="/wiki/Brood_patch" title="Brood patch">brood patch</a> of bare skin and plays the main part in <a href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian incubation">incubating</a> the eggs. The male helps, but can only cover the eggs rather than truly incubate them. The female spends the night incubating during this period, while the male roosts near the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988148–149_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988148–149-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Eggs hatch at the same time, after a short incubation period lasting 11–14&#160;days, and exceptionally for as many as 17 or as few as 9.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147_132-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988144–147-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The length of the incubation period decreases as ambient temperature increases later in the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988149–150_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988149–150-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_detail(loz).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1352" data-file-height="1086" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_detail(loz).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A female feeding a fledgling</div></div></div>
<p>Young house sparrows remain in the nest for 11 to 23 days, normally 14 to 16 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_107-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988149–150_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988149–150-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer199760ff_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer199760ff-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> During this time, they are fed by both parents. As newly hatched house sparrows do not have sufficient insulation, they are brooded for a few days, or longer in cold conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988149–150_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988149–150-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer1997105–115_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer1997105–115-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The parents swallow the droppings produced by the hatchlings during the first few days; later, the droppings are moved up to 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft) away from the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer1997105–115_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer1997105–115-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The chicks' eyes open after about 4 days and, at an age of about 8 days, the young birds get their first <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">down</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_107-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer199760ff_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer199760ff-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> If both parents perish, the ensuing intensive begging sounds of the young often attract replacement parents which feed them until they can sustain themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer1997105–115_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer1997105–115-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> All the young in the nest leave it during the same period of a few hours. At this stage, they are normally able to fly. They start feeding themselves partly after 1 or 2 days, and sustain themselves completely after 7 to 10 days, 14 at the latest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer199779–89_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlutz_von_BlotzheimBauer199779–89-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Survival">Survival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Survival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In adult house sparrows, annual survival is 45–65%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988154–155_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988154–155-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> After fledging and leaving the care of their parents, young sparrows have a high mortality rate, which lessens as they grow older and more experienced. Only about 20–25% of birds hatched survive to their first breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–141_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988137–141-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The oldest known wild house sparrow lived for nearly two decades; it was found dead 19&#160;years and 9&#160;months after it was <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> in Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The oldest recorded captive house sparrow lived for 23&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The typical ratio of males to females in a population is uncertain due to problems in collecting data, but a very slight preponderance of males at all ages is usual.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006333–336_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006333–336-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predation">Predation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Predation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cat-eating-prey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cat-eating-prey.jpg/220px-Cat-eating-prey.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cat-eating-prey.jpg/330px-Cat-eating-prey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cat-eating-prey.jpg/440px-Cat-eating-prey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cat-eating-prey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A male being eaten by a cat: <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">Domestic cats</a> are one of the main predators of the house sparrow.</div></div></div>
<p>The house sparrow's main predators are <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">birds of prey</a>, but many other animals prey on them, including <a href="/wiki/Corvidae" title="Corvidae">corvids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006304–306_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006304–306-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> and even humans—the house sparrow has been consumed in the past by people in many parts of the world, and it still is in parts of the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith199230–33_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith199230–33-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Most species of birds of prey have been recorded preying on the house sparrow in places where records are extensive. <a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiters</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlin</a> in particular are major predators, though cats are likely to have a greater impact on house sparrow populations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006304–306_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006304–306-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow is also a common victim of <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">roadkill</a>; on European roads, it is the bird most frequently found dead.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites_and_disease">Parasites and disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Parasites and disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow is host to a huge number of parasites and diseases, and the effect of most is unknown. Ornithologist Ted R. Anderson listed thousands, noting that his list was incomplete.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006311–317_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006311–317-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The commonly recorded bacterial pathogens of the house sparrow are often those common in humans, and include <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963128_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963128-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> <i>Salmonella</i> is common in the house sparrow, and a comprehensive study of house sparrow disease found it in 13% of sparrows tested. <i>Salmonella</i> epidemics in the spring and winter can kill large numbers of sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006311–317_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006311–317-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The house sparrow hosts <a href="/wiki/Avian_pox" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian pox">avian pox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">avian malaria</a>, which it has spread to the native forest birds of Hawaii.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Many of the diseases hosted by the house sparrow are also present in humans and domestic animals, for which the house sparrow acts as a <a href="/wiki/Natural_reservoir" title="Natural reservoir">reservoir host</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006427–429_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006427–429-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arbovirus_infection" class="mw-redirect" title="Arbovirus infection">Arboviruses</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a>, which most commonly infect insects and mammals, survive winters in temperate areas by going dormant in birds such as the house sparrow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006311–317_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006311–317-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> A few records indicate disease extirpating house sparrow populations, especially from Scottish islands, but this seems to be rare.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963129_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963129-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> House sparrows are also infected by <a href="/wiki/Haemosporida" title="Haemosporida">haemosporidian</a> parasites, but less so in urban than in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The house sparrow is infested by a number of external parasites, which usually cause little harm to adult sparrows. In Europe, the most common <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mite</a> found on sparrows is <i><a href="/wiki/Proctophyllodes" title="Proctophyllodes">Proctophyllodes</a></i>, the most common <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> are <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argas_reflexus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Argas reflexus (page does not exist)">Argas reflexus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ixodes_arboricola" title="Ixodes arboricola">Ixodes arboricola</a></i>, and the most common flea on the house sparrow is <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratophyllus_gallinae" title="Ceratophyllus gallinae">Ceratophyllus gallinae</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963131–132_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963131–132-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Dermanyssus" title="Dermanyssus">Dermanyssus</a></i> blood-feeding mites are also common ectoparasites of house sparrows,<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> and these mites can enter human habitation and bite humans, causing a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Gamasoidosis" title="Gamasoidosis">gamasoidosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> A number of <a href="/wiki/Chewing_louse" class="mw-redirect" title="Chewing louse">chewing lice</a> occupy different <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niches</a> on the house sparrow's body. <i><a href="/wiki/Menacanthus" title="Menacanthus">Menacanthus</a></i> lice occur across the house sparrow's body, where they feed on blood and feathers, while <i><a href="/wiki/Brueelia" title="Brueelia">Brueelia</a></i> lice feed on feathers and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Philopterus_fringillae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Philopterus fringillae (page does not exist)">Philopterus fringillae</a></i> occurs on the head.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963131–132_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1963131–132-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG/220px-Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG/330px-Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG/440px-Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alpenquai_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Passer_domesticus_2014-09-15_17-37-56.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An immature house sparrow sleeping</div></div></div>
<p>House sparrows express strong <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">circadian rhythms</a> of activity in the laboratory. They were among the first bird species to be seriously studied in terms of their circadian activity and <a href="/wiki/Photoperiodism" title="Photoperiodism">photoperiodism</a>, in part because of their availability and adaptability in captivity, but also because they can "find their way" and remain rhythmic in constant darkness.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Such studies have found that the <a href="/wiki/Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland">pineal gland</a> is a central part of the house sparrow's circadian system: removal of the pineal eliminates the circadian rhythm of activity,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> and transplant of the pineal into another individual confers to this individual the rhythm phase of the donor bird.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Suprachiasmatic_nuclei" class="mw-redirect" title="Suprachiasmatic nuclei">suprachiasmatic nuclei</a> of the hypothalamus have also been shown to be an important component of the circadian system of house sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The photoreceptors involved in the synchronisation of the circadian clock to the external light-dark cycle are located in the brain and can be stimulated by light reaching them directly though the skull, as revealed by experiments in which blind sparrows, which normally can still synchronise to the light-dark cycle, failed to do so once India ink was injected as a screen under the skin on top of their skulls.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, even when blind, house sparrows continue to be photoperiodic, i.e. show reproductive development when the days are long, but not when the days are short. This response is stronger when the feathers on top of the head are plucked, and is eliminated when India ink is injected under the skin at the top of the head, showing that the photoreceptors involved in the photoperiodic response to day length are located inside the brain.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>House sparrows have also been used in studies of nonphotic entrainment (i.e. synchronisation to an external cycle other than light and dark): for example, in constant darkness, a situation in which the birds would normally reveal their endogenous, non-24-hour, "free-running" rhythms of activity, they instead show 24-hour periodicity if they are exposed to two hours of chirp playbacks every 24&#160;hours, matching their daily activity onsets with the daily playback onsets.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> House sparrows in constant dim light can also be entrained to a daily cycle based on the presence of food.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Finally, house sparrows in constant darkness could be entrained to a cycle of high and low temperature, but only if the difference in temperature was large (38&#160;°C versus 6&#160;°C); some of the tested sparrows matched their activity to the warm phase, and others to the cold phase.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships_with_humans">Relationships with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Relationships with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_10" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:640px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_11&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;676880&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Passer domesticus - gathering at fluorescent tube.ogv" style="width:220px;height:165px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogv/220px--Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flocking and chirping together beneath a <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_tube" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent tube">fluorescent tube</a> light in Germany</div></div></div>
<p>The house sparrow is closely associated with humans. They are believed to have become associated with humans around 10,000 years ago. The Turkestan subspecies (<i>P. d. bactrianus</i>) is least associated with humans and considered to be evolutionarily closer to the ancestral noncommensal populations.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Usually, the house sparrow is regarded as a pest, since it consumes agricultural products and spreads disease to humans and their domestic animals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006425–429_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006425–429-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Even <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">birdwatchers</a> often hold it in little regard because of its molestation of other birds.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_77-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In most of the world, the house sparrow is not protected by law. Attempts to control house sparrows include the trapping, poisoning, or shooting of adults; the destruction of their nests and eggs; or less directly, blocking nest holes and scaring off sparrows with noise, glue, or porcupine wire.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSG_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSG-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> However, the house sparrow can be beneficial to humans, as well, especially by eating insect pests, and attempts at the large-scale control of the house sparrow have failed.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The house sparrow has long been used as a food item. From around 1560 to at least the 19th century in northern Europe, <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a> "sparrow pots" were hung from eaves to attract nesting birds so  the young could be readily harvested. Wild birds were trapped in nets in large numbers, and sparrow pie was a traditional dish, thought, because of the association of sparrows with <a href="/wiki/Lascivious#Lechery" class="mw-redirect" title="Lascivious">lechery</a>, to have <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiac</a> properties. Sparrows were also trapped as food for <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconers'</a> birds and zoo animals. During the 1870s, there were debates on the damaging effects of sparrows in the House of Commons in England.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>  In the early part of the 20th century, sparrow clubs culled many millions of birds and eggs in an attempt to control numbers of this perceived pest, but with only a localised impact on numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECockerMabey2005436–443_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECockerMabey2005436–443-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> House sparrows have been kept as pets at many times in history, though they have no bright plumage or attractive songs, and raising them is difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith200529–35_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith200529–35-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The house sparrow has an extremely large range and population, and is not seriously threatened by human activities, so it is assessed as <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> for conservation on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> estimates for the global population runs up to nearly 1.4 billion individuals, second among all birds perhaps only to the <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_quelea" title="Red-billed quelea">red-billed quelea</a> in abundance (although the quelea is, unlike the sparrow, restricted to a single continent and has never been subject to human introductions).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> However, populations have been declining in many parts of the world, especially near its Eurasian places of origin.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> These declines were first noticed in North America, where they were initially attributed to the spread of the <a href="/wiki/House_finch" title="House finch">house finch</a>, but have been most severe in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006320_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006320-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Declines have not been universal, as no serious declines have been reported from <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, but have even occurred in Australia, where the house sparrow was introduced recently.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006229–300_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2006229–300-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, populations peaked in the early 1970s,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988157–158,_296_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988157–158,_296-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> but have since declined by 68% overall,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> and about 90% in some regions.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> The RSPB lists the house sparrow's UK conservation status as red.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> In London, the house sparrow almost disappeared from the central city.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> The numbers of house sparrows in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> have dropped in half since the 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-Poel_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poel-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> so the house sparrow is even considered an <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> This status came to widespread attention after a female house sparrow, referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Domino_Day_2005_sparrow" title="Domino Day 2005 sparrow">Dominomus</a>", was killed after knocking down dominoes arranged as part of an attempt to set a world record.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> These declines are not unprecedented, as similar reductions in population occurred when the internal combustion engine replaced horses in the 1920s and a major source of food in the form of grain spillage was lost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988156_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith1988156-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Various causes for the dramatic decreases in population have been proposed, including predation, in particular by <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawks</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy3_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy3-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> electromagnetic radiation from mobile phones;<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> and diseases<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> such as avian malaria.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> A shortage of nesting sites caused by changes in urban building design is probably a factor, and conservation organisations have encouraged the use of special nest boxes for sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> A primary cause of the decline seems to be an insufficient supply of insect food for nestling sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2_220-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Declines in insect populations result from an increase of monoculture crops, the heavy use of pesticides,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVincent2005265–270_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVincent2005265–270-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> the replacement of native plants in cities with introduced plants and parking areas,<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> and possibly the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Unleaded_petrol" class="mw-redirect" title="Unleaded petrol">unleaded petrol</a>, which produces toxic compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Methyl_nitrite" title="Methyl nitrite">methyl nitrite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Protecting insect habitats on farms,<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> and planting native plants in cities benefit the house sparrow, as does establishing urban green spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-Leylandii_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leylandii-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> To raise awareness of threats to the house sparrow, <a href="/wiki/World_Sparrow_Day" title="World Sparrow Day">World Sparrow Day</a> has been celebrated on 20 March across the world since 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
Over the recent years, the house sparrow population has been on the decline in many Asian countries, and this decline is quite evident in India. To promote the conservation of these birds, in 2012, the house sparrow was declared as the state bird of Delhi.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_associations">Cultural associations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Cultural associations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To many people across the world, the house sparrow is the most familiar wild animal and, because of its association with humans and familiarity, it is frequently used to represent the common and vulgar, or the lewd.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> One of the reasons for the introduction of house sparrows throughout the world was their association with the European homeland of many immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-Healy_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Healy-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Birds usually described later as sparrows are referred to in many works of ancient literature and religious texts in Europe and western Asia. These references may not always refer specifically to the house sparrow, or even to small, seed-eating birds, but later writers who were inspired by these texts often had the house sparrow in mind.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> In particular, sparrows were associated by the ancient Greeks with <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>, the goddess of love, due to their perceived lustfulness, an association echoed by later writers such as <a href="/wiki/Chaucer" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaucer">Chaucer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_39-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith200529–35_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith200529–35-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215_238-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Jesus's use of "sparrows" as an example of divine providence in the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a><sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> also inspired later references, such as that in Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215_238-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers-Smith196349,_215-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">Gospel</a> <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn</a> <i><a href="/wiki/His_Eye_Is_on_the_Sparrow" title="His Eye Is on the Sparrow">His Eye Is on the Sparrow</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd201256–58_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd201256–58-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="float:right">
<table class="mw-hiero-table mw-hiero-outer" dir="ltr"><tbody><tr><td> <table class="mw-hiero-table"><tbody><tr> <td><img style="margin: 1px;" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_G37.png?ab9e8" height="28" title="G37" alt="G37" /></td></tr></tbody></table> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<div style="display:inline;"> The house sparrow is represented in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Egyptian art">ancient Egyptian art</a> very rarely, but an <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyph" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian hieroglyph">Egyptian hieroglyph</a> is based on it. The sparrow hieroglyph had no phonetic value and was used as a determinative in words to indicate small, narrow, or bad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoulihanGoodman1986136–137_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoulihanGoodman1986136–137-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> An alternative view is that the hieroglyph meant "a prolific man" or "the revolution of a year".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson1847211–212_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson1847211–212-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="noprint metadata navbox" role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" style="font-weight:bold;padding:0.4em 2em"><ul style="margin:0.1em 0 0"><li style="display:inline"><span style="display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap"><span style="margin:0 0.5em"><a href="/wiki/File:Eagle_01.svg" class="image"><img alt="Eagle 01.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Eagle_01.svg/17px-Eagle_01.svg.png" decoding="async" width="17" height="21" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Eagle_01.svg/27px-Eagle_01.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Eagle_01.svg/35px-Eagle_01.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="317" data-file-height="381" /></a></span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Birds" title="Portal:Birds">Birds portal</a></span></li></ul></div>
